      <p><b>Equatorial Guinea</b> (<a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i lang="es">Guinea Ecuatorial</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[a]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Guinée équatoriale</i>; <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>: <i lang="pt">Guiné Equatorial</i>), officially the <b>Republic of Equatorial Guinea</b> (Spanish: <i lang="es">República de Guinea Ecuatorial</i>, French: <i lang="fr">République de Guinée équatoriale</i>, Portuguese: <i lang="pt">República da Guiné Equatorial</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[b]</a></sup> is a country located on the west coast of <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>, with an area of 28,000 square kilometres (11,000&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi). Formerly the colony of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Guinea" title="Spanish Guinea">Spanish Guinea</a>, its post-independence name evokes its location near both the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a>. As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the country had a population of 1,225,367.<sup id="cite_ref-AnuarioEstadistico2018_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AnuarioEstadistico2018-8">[8]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Equatorial Guinea consists of two parts, an insular and a mainland region. The <a href="/wiki/Islands_of_Equatorial_Guinea" class="mw-redirect" title="Islands of Equatorial Guinea">insular region</a> consists of the islands of <a href="/wiki/Bioko" title="Bioko">Bioko</a> (formerly <i>Fernando Pó</i>) in the Gulf of Guinea and <a href="/wiki/Annob%C3%B3n_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Annobón Province">Annobón</a>, a small volcanic island which is the only part of the country south of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. Bioko Island is the northernmost part of Equatorial Guinea and is the site of the country's capital, <a href="/wiki/Malabo" title="Malabo">Malabo</a>. The Portuguese-speaking island nation of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="São Tomé and Príncipe">São Tomé and Príncipe</a> is located between Bioko and Annobón. The mainland region, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Muni" title="Río Muni">Río Muni</a>, is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> on the north and <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a> on the south and east. It is the location of <a href="/wiki/Bata,_Equatorial_Guinea" title="Bata, Equatorial Guinea">Bata</a>, Equatorial Guinea's largest city, and <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_de_la_Paz" title="Ciudad de la Paz">Ciudad de la Paz</a>, the country's planned future capital. Rio Muni also includes several small offshore islands, such as <a href="/wiki/Corisco" title="Corisco">Corisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elobey_Grande" title="Elobey Grande">Elobey Grande</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Elobey_Chico" title="Elobey Chico">Elobey Chico</a>. The country is a member of the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organisation_internationale_de_la_Francophonie" title="Organisation internationale de la Francophonie">Francophonie</a>, <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Portuguese_Language_Countries" title="Community of Portuguese Language Countries">CPLP</a>. </p>
      <p>After becoming independent from Spain in 1968, Equatorial Guinea was ruled by President <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Mac%C3%ADas_Nguema" title="Francisco Macías Nguema">Francisco Macías Nguema</a> until he was overthrown in a <a href="/wiki/1979_Equatorial_Guinea_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1979 Equatorial Guinea coup d'état">coup in 1979</a> by his nephew <a href="/wiki/Teodoro_Obiang_Nguema_Mbasogo" title="Teodoro Obiang Nguema Mbasogo">Teodoro Obiang Nguema Mbasogo</a> who has served as the country's President since. Both presidents have been widely characterized as dictators by foreign observers. Since the mid-1990s, Equatorial Guinea has become one of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>'s largest oil producers.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> It has subsequently become the richest country per capita in Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup> and its <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) adjusted for <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> (PPP) per capita ranks 43rd in the world;<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> however, the wealth is distributed extremely unevenly, with few people benefiting from the oil riches. The country ranks 144th on the 2019 <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-HDI_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI-16">[14]</a></sup> with less than half the population having access to clean drinking water and 20% of children dying before the age of five. </p>
      <p>Equatorial Guinea's government is authoritarian and has one of the <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Equatorial_Guinea" title="Human rights in Equatorial Guinea">worst human rights records in the world</a>, consistently ranking among the "worst of the worst" in <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Freedom_in_the_World" title="Freedom in the World">annual survey of political and civil rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Reporters_Without_Borders" title="Reporters Without Borders">Reporters Without Borders</a> ranks President Obiang among its "predators" of press freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking_in_Equatorial_Guinea" title="Human trafficking in Equatorial Guinea">Human trafficking</a> is a significant problem; the 2012 U.S. Trafficking in Persons Report stated that Equatorial Guinea "is a source and destination for women and children subjected to <a href="/wiki/Unfree_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Unfree labor">forced labour</a> and forced sex trafficking." The report rates Equatorial Guinea as a government that "does not fully comply with minimum standards and is not making significant efforts to do so."<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Equatorial_Guinea" title="History of Equatorial Guinea">History of Equatorial Guinea</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Pygmy_peoples" title="Pygmy peoples">Pygmies</a> probably once lived in the continental region that is now Equatorial Guinea, but are today found only in isolated pockets in southern Río Muni. <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a> migrations started probably around 2,000 BC from between south-east Nigeria and north-west Cameroon (the Grassfields).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> They must have settled continental Equatorial Guinea around 500 BC at the latest.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> The earliest settlements on Bioko Island are dated to AD 530.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Annobonese_Creole" title="Annobonese Creole">Annobón</a> population, originally native to <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>, was introduced by the Portuguese via <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_island" class="mw-redirect" title="São Tomé island">São Tomé island</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="First_European_contact_and_Portuguese_rule_.281472.E2.80.931778.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_European_contact_and_Portuguese_rule_(1472–1778)">First European contact and Portuguese rule (1472–1778)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: First European contact and Portuguese rule (1472–1778)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:1729_West_Africa_map_(Cameroon_%26_Nigeria).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/1729_West_Africa_map_%28Cameroon_%26_Nigeria%29.jpg/220px-1729_West_Africa_map_%28Cameroon_%26_Nigeria%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/1729_West_Africa_map_%28Cameroon_%26_Nigeria%29.jpg/330px-1729_West_Africa_map_%28Cameroon_%26_Nigeria%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/1729_West_Africa_map_%28Cameroon_%26_Nigeria%29.jpg/440px-1729_West_Africa_map_%28Cameroon_%26_Nigeria%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="355"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:1729_West_Africa_map_(Cameroon_%26_Nigeria).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> rule in Equatorial Guinea lasted from the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Fern%C3%A3o_do_P%C3%B3" title="Fernão do Pó">Fernão do Pó</a> (Fernando Pó) in 1472 until the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_El_Pardo_(1778)" title="Treaty of El Pardo (1778)">1778 Treaty of El Pardo</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese explorer</a> <a href="/wiki/Fern%C3%A3o_do_P%C3%B3" title="Fernão do Pó">Fernando Pó</a>, seeking a path to <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, is credited as being the first European to discover the island of Bioko, in 1472. He called it <i>Formosa</i> ("Beautiful"), but it quickly took on the name of its European discoverer. <a href="/wiki/Fernando_P%C3%B3_(island)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fernando Pó (island)">Fernando Pó</a> and Annobón were colonized by <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> in 1474. The first factories were established on the islands around 1500 as the Portuguese quickly recognized the positives of the islands including volcanic soil and disease-resistant highlands. Despite natural advantages, initial Portuguese efforts in 1507 to establish a sugarcane plantation and town near what is now Concepción on Fernando Pó failed due to Bubi hostility and fever.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> The main island's rainy climate, extreme humidity and temperature swings took a major toll on European settlers from the beginning, and it would be centuries before attempts restarted.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Early_Spanish_rule_and_lease_to_Britain_.281778.E2.80.931844.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Spanish_rule_and_lease_to_Britain_(1778–1844)">Early Spanish rule and lease to Britain (1778–1844)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early Spanish rule and lease to Britain (1778–1844)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In 1778, Queen <a href="/wiki/Maria_I_of_Portugal" title="Maria I of Portugal">Maria I of Portugal</a> and King <a href="/wiki/Charles_III_of_Spain" title="Charles III of Spain">Charles III of Spain</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_El_Pardo_(1778)" title="Treaty of El Pardo (1778)">Treaty of El Pardo</a> which ceded <a href="/wiki/Bioko" title="Bioko">Bioko</a>, adjacent islets, and commercial rights to the <a href="/wiki/Bight_of_Bonny" class="mw-redirect" title="Bight of Bonny">Bight of Biafra</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ogoue_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Ogoue River">Ogoue</a> rivers to <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spain</a>. Brigadier Felipe José, Count of Arjelejos sailed from <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a> to formally take possession of Bioko from Portugal, landing on the island on 21 October 1778. After sailing for Annobón to take possession, the Count died of disease caught on Bioko and the fever-ridden crew mutinied. The crew landed on São Tomé instead where they were imprisoned by the Portuguese authorities after having lost over 80% of their men to sickness.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> As a result of this disaster, Spain was thereafter hesitant to invest heavily in their new possession. However, despite the setback Spaniards began to use the island as a base for slave trading on the nearby mainland. Between 1778 and 1810, the territory of what became Equatorial Guinea was administered by the <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_the_R%C3%ADo_de_la_Plata" title="Viceroyalty of the Río de la Plata">Viceroyalty of the Río de la Plata</a>, based in <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Unwilling to invest heavily in the development of Fernando Pó, from 1827 to 1843, the Spanish leased a base at Malabo on <a href="/wiki/Bioko" title="Bioko">Bioko</a> to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a> which they had sought as part of their efforts to suppress the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">transatlantic slave trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> Without Spanish permission, the British moved the headquarters of the Mixed Commission for the Suppression of Slave Traffic to Fernando Pó in 1827, before moving it back to <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> under an agreement with Spain in 1843. Spain's decision to abolish slavery in 1817 at British insistence damaged the colony's perceived value to the authorities and so leasing naval bases was an effective revenue earner from an otherwise unprofitable possession.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> An agreement by Spain to sell their African colony to the British was cancelled in 1841 due to metropolitan public opinion and opposition by Spanish Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Late_19th_century_.281844.E2.80.931900.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_19th_century_(1844–1900)">Late 19th century (1844–1900)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Late 19th century (1844–1900)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:(1897)_Golfo_de_Guinea.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/%281897%29_Golfo_de_Guinea.jpg/300px-%281897%29_Golfo_de_Guinea.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="429" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/%281897%29_Golfo_de_Guinea.jpg/450px-%281897%29_Golfo_de_Guinea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/%281897%29_Golfo_de_Guinea.jpg/600px-%281897%29_Golfo_de_Guinea.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2473" data-file-height="3535"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:(1897)_Golfo_de_Guinea.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map of the Spanish possessions in 1897
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1844, the British returned the island to Spanish control and the area became known as the "Territorios Españoles del Golfo de Guinea." Due to epidemics Spain did not invest much in the colony, and in 1862 an outbreak of yellow fever killed many of the whites that had settled on the island. Despite this, plantations continued to be established by private citizens through the second half of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-30">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bioko" title="Bioko">Fernando Pó</a> were mostly run by a black <a href="/wiki/Creole_elite" class="mw-redirect" title="Creole elite">Creole elite</a>, later known as <a href="/wiki/Fernandino_peoples" title="Fernandino peoples">Fernandinos</a>. The British settled some 2,000 Sierra Leoneans and freed slaves there during their rule, and a trickle of immigration from West Africa and the West Indies continued after the British left. A number of freed Angolan slaves, Portuguese-African creoles and immigrants from Nigeria and Liberia also began to be settled in the colony where they quickly began to join the new group.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-31">[29]</a></sup> To the local mix were added Cubans, Filipinos, Jews and Spaniards of various colours, many of who had deported to Africa for political or other crimes, as well as some settlers backed by the government.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By 1870 the prognosis of whites that lived on the island was much improved after recommendations that they live in the highlands, and by 1884 much of the minimal administrative machinery and key plantations had moved to <a href="/wiki/Pico_Basil%C3%A9" title="Pico Basilé">Basile</a> hundreds of meters above sea level. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a> had labeled Fernando Pó "a jewel which Spain did not polish" for refusing to enact such a policy. Despite the improved survival chances of Europeans living on the island, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Kingsley" title="Mary Kingsley">Mary Kingsley</a>, who was staying on the island still described Fernando Pó as 'a more uncomfortable form of execution' for Spaniards appointed there.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-30">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There was also a trickle of immigration from the neighboring Portuguese islands, escaped slaves, and prospective planters. Although a few of the <a href="/wiki/Fernandino_peoples" title="Fernandino peoples">Fernandinos</a> were Catholic and Spanish-speaking, about nine-tenths of them were Protestant and English-speaking on the eve of the First World War, and <a href="/wiki/Pidgin_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Pidgin English">pidgin English</a> was the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the island. The Sierra Leoneans were particularly well placed as planters while labor recruitment on the <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Windward coast</a> continued, for they kept family and other connections there and could easily arrange a supply of labor. The Fernandinos proved to become effective traders and middlemen between the natives and Europeans.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-31">[29]</a></sup> A freed slave from the West Indies by way of Sierra Leone named William Pratt established the cocoa crop on Fernando Pó, forever altering the destiny of the colony.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Early_20th_century_.281900.E2.80.931945.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century_(1900–1945)">Early 20th century (1900–1945)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early 20th century (1900–1945)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Spain had not occupied the large area in the <a href="/wiki/Bight_of_Biafra" title="Bight of Biafra">Bight of Biafra</a> to which it had right by <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaty</a>, and the French had busily expanded their occupation at the expense of the territory claimed by Spain. Madrid only partly backed the explorations of men like <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Iradier" title="Manuel Iradier">Manuel Iradier</a> who had signed treaties in the interior as far as Gabon and Cameroon, leaving much of the land out of 'effective occupation' as demanded by the terms of the 1885 <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference</a>. More important events such as the conflict in Cuba and the eventual <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a> kept Madrid busy at an inopportune moment. Minimal government backing for mainland annexation came as a result of public opinion and a need for labour on Fernando Pó.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The eventual <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1900)" title="Treaty of Paris (1900)">treaty of Paris</a> in 1900 left Spain with the continental <a href="/wiki/Enclave_and_exclave" title="Enclave and exclave">enclave</a> of Rio Muni, a mere 26,000&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> out of the 300,000 stretching east to the <a href="/wiki/Ubangi_River" title="Ubangi River">Ubangi river</a> which the Spaniards had initially claimed.<sup id="cite_ref-Clarence-Smith_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarence-Smith-34">[32]</a></sup> The tiny enclave was far smaller than what the Spaniards had considered themselves rightfully entitled to under their claims and the Treaty of El Pardo. The humiliation of the Franco-Spanish negotiations, combined with the disaster in Cuba led to the head of the Spanish negotiating team, Pedro Gover y Tovar committing suicide on the voyage home on 21 October 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-35">[33]</a></sup> Iradier himself died in despair in 1911, and it would be decades before his achievements would be recognised by Spanish popular opinion when the port of <a href="/wiki/Cogo,_Equatorial_Guinea" title="Cogo, Equatorial Guinea">Cogo</a> was renamed Puerto Iradier in his honour.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The opening years of the twentieth century saw a new generation of Spanish immigrants. Land regulations issued in 1904–1905 favoured Spaniards, and most of the later big planters arrived from Spain after that. An agreement made with Liberia in 1914 to import cheap labor greatly favoured wealthy men with ready access to the state, and the shift in labor supplies from Liberia to Río Muni increased this advantage. Due to malpractice however, the Liberian government eventually ended the treaty after embarrassing revelations about the state of Liberian workers on Fernando Pó in the Christy Report which brought down the country's president <a href="/wiki/Charles_D._B._King" title="Charles D. B. King">Charles D. B. King</a> in 1930. In 1940, an estimated 20% of the colony's cocoa production came from African-owned land, nearly all of it was in the hands of Fernandinos.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Corisco-Saliendo_de_misa-1910.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Corisco-Saliendo_de_misa-1910.jpg/220px-Corisco-Saliendo_de_misa-1910.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Corisco-Saliendo_de_misa-1910.jpg/330px-Corisco-Saliendo_de_misa-1910.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Corisco-Saliendo_de_misa-1910.jpg/440px-Corisco-Saliendo_de_misa-1910.jpg 2x" data-file-width="489" data-file-height="304"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Corisco-Saliendo_de_misa-1910.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Corisco" title="Corisco">Corisco</a>, 1910
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The greatest constraint to <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a> was a chronic shortage of labour. Pushed into the interior of the island and decimated by alcohol addiction, venereal disease, <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/African_trypanosomiasis" title="African trypanosomiasis">sleeping sickness</a>, the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Bubi_people" title="Bubi people">Bubi</a> population of <a href="/wiki/Bioko" title="Bioko">Bioko</a> refused to work on <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantations</a>. Working their own small <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa</a> farms gave them a considerable degree of autonomy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>By the late nineteenth century, the Bubi were protected from the demands of the planters by Spanish <a href="/wiki/Claretians" title="Claretians">Claretian</a> missionaries, who were very influential in the colony and eventually organised the Bubi into little mission theocracies reminiscent of the famous <a href="/wiki/Jesuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Reducciones" class="mw-redirect" title="Reducciones">reductions</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>. Catholic penetration was furthered by two small insurrections in 1898 and 1910 protesting <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> of <a href="/wiki/Forced_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced labor">forced labour</a> for the plantations. The Bubi were disarmed in 1917, and left dependent on the missionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Clarence-Smith_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarence-Smith-34">[32]</a></sup> Serious labour shortages were temporarily solved by a massive influx of refugees from German <a href="/wiki/Kamerun" title="Kamerun">Kamerun</a>, along with thousands of white German soldiers who stayed on the island for several years.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-35">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Between 1926 and 1959 Bioko and Rio Muni were united as the colony of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Guinea" title="Spanish Guinea">Spanish Guinea</a>. The economy was based on large <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cacao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> plantations and <a href="/wiki/Logging" title="Logging">logging</a> concessions and the workforce was mostly immigrant <a href="/wiki/Contract_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Contract labour">contract labour</a> from <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cameroun" class="mw-redirect" title="Cameroun">Cameroun</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> Between 1914 and 1930, an estimated 10,000 Liberians went to Fernando Po under a labour treaty that was stopped altogether in 1930.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>With Liberian workers no longer available, planters of Fernando Po turned to Rio Muni. Campaigns were mounted to subdue the <a href="/wiki/Fang_people" title="Fang people">Fang people</a> in the 1920s, at the time that Liberia was beginning to cut back on recruitment. There were garrisons of the colonial guard throughout the enclave by 1926, and the whole colony was considered 'pacified' by 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> had a major impact on the colony. 150 Spanish whites, including the Governor-General and Vice-Governor-General of Río Muni created a socialist party called the Popular Front in the enclave which served to oppose the interests of the Fernando Pó plantation owners. When the War broke out <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a> ordered Nationalist forces based in the Canaries to ensure control over Equatorial Guinea. In September 1936 Nationalist forces backed by Falangists from Fernando Pó, similarly to what happened in Spain proper took control of Río Muni, which under Governor-General Luiz Sanchez Guerra Saez and his deputy Porcel had backed the Republican government. By November the Popular Front and its supporters had been defeated and Equatorial Guinea secured for Franco. The commander in charge of the occupation, Juan Fontán Lobé was appointed Governor-General by Franco and began to exert more effective Spanish control over the enclave interior.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Rio Muni had a small population, officially a little over 100,000 in the 1930s, and escape across the frontiers into <a href="/wiki/Cameroun" class="mw-redirect" title="Cameroun">Cameroun</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a> was very easy. Also, the <a href="/wiki/Timber" class="mw-redirect" title="Timber">timber</a> companies needed increasing numbers of workers, and the spread of <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> cultivation offered an alternative means of paying taxes<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (November 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. Fernando Pó thus continued to suffer from labour shortages. The French only briefly permitted recruitment in Cameroun, and the main source of labour came to be <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a> smuggled in canoes from <a href="/wiki/Calabar" title="Calabar">Calabar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>. This resolution to the worker shortage allowed Fernando Pó to become one of Africa's most productive agricultural areas after the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clarence-Smith_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarence-Smith-34">[32]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Final_years_of_Spanish_rule_.281945.E2.80.931968.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_of_Spanish_rule_(1945–1968)">Final years of Spanish rule (1945–1968)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Final years of Spanish rule (1945–1968)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Centro_Cultural_de_Espa%C3%B1a_en_Malabo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Centro_Cultural_de_Espa%C3%B1a_en_Malabo.jpg/300px-Centro_Cultural_de_Espa%C3%B1a_en_Malabo.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Centro_Cultural_de_Espa%C3%B1a_en_Malabo.jpg/450px-Centro_Cultural_de_Espa%C3%B1a_en_Malabo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Centro_Cultural_de_Espa%C3%B1a_en_Malabo.jpg/600px-Centro_Cultural_de_Espa%C3%B1a_en_Malabo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1327" data-file-height="885"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Centro_Cultural_de_Espa%C3%B1a_en_Malabo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Centro Cultural de España (Cultural Center of Spain in Malabo)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Politically, post-war colonial history has three fairly distinct phases: up to 1959, when its status was raised from 'colonial' to 'provincial', following the approach of the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>; between 1960 and 1968, when Madrid attempted a partial <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Decolonisation">decolonisation</a> aimed at keeping the territory as part of the Spanish system; and from 1968 on, after the territory became an independent <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>. The first phase consisted of little more than a continuation of previous policies; these closely resembled the policies of Portugal and France, notably in dividing the population into a vast majority governed as 'natives' or non-citizens, and a very small minority (together with whites) admitted to civic status as <i><a href="/wiki/Emancipados" title="Emancipados">emancipados</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilation</a> to the metropolitan culture being the only permissible means of advancement.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>This 'provincial' phase saw the beginnings of <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, but chiefly among small groups who had taken refuge from the <i><a href="/wiki/Caudillo" title="Caudillo">Caudillo</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s paternal hand in Cameroun and Gabon. They formed two bodies: the <a href="/wiki/Atanasio_Ndongo_Miyone" title="Atanasio Ndongo Miyone">Movimiento Nacional de Liberación de la Guinea</a> (MONALIGE), and the <a href="/wiki/Idea_Popular_de_Guinea_Ecuatorial" class="mw-redirect" title="Idea Popular de Guinea Ecuatorial">Idea Popular de Guinea Ecuatorial</a> (IPGE). The pressure they could bring to bear was weak, but the general trend in West Africa was not, and by the late 1960s much of the African continent had been granted independence. Aware of this trend, the Spanish began to increase efforts to prepare the country for independence and massively stepped up development. The Gross National Product per capita in 1965 was $466 which was the highest in black Africa, and the Spanish constructed an international airport at Santa Isabel, a television station and increased the literacy rate to a relatively high 89%. At the same time measures were taken to battle sleeping sickness and leprosy in the enclave, and by 1967 the number of hospital beds per capita in Equatorial Guinea was higher than Spain itself, with 1637 beds in 16 hospitals. All the same, measures to improve education floundered and like in the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a> by the end of colonial rule the number of Africans in higher education was in only the double digits, and political education necessary to a functioning state was negligible.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A decision of 9 August 1963, approved by a referendum of 15 December 1963, gave the territory a measure of autonomy and the administrative promotion of a 'moderate' group, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Movimiento_de_Uni%C3%B3n_Nacional_de_Guinea_Ecuatorial&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Movimiento de Unión Nacional de Guinea Ecuatorial (page does not exist)">Movimiento de Unión Nacional de Guinea Ecuatorial</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&nbsp;[<a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Movimiento_de_Uni%C3%B3n_Nacional_de_Guinea_Ecuatorial" class="extiw" title="es:Movimiento de Unión Nacional de Guinea Ecuatorial">es</a>]</span> (MUNGE). This proved a feeble instrument, and, with growing pressure for change from the UN, Madrid was gradually forced to give way to the currents of nationalism. Two General Assembly resolutions were passed in 1965 ordering Spain to grant independence to the colony, and in 1966 a UN Commission toured the country before recommending the same thing. In response, the Spanish declared that they would hold a constitutional convention on 27 October 1967 to negotiate a new constitution for an independent Equatorial Guinea. The conference was attended by 41 local delegates and 25 Spaniards. The Africans were principally divided between Fernandinos and Bubi on one side, who feared a loss of privileges and 'swamping' by the Fang majority, and the Río Muni Fang nationalists on the other. At the conference the leading Fang figure, the later first president <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Mac%C3%ADas_Nguema" title="Francisco Macías Nguema">Francisco Macías Nguema</a> gave a controversial speech in which he claimed that <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> had 'saved Africa'.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> After nine sessions the conference was suspended due to deadlock between the 'unionists' and 'separatists' who wanted a separate Fernando Pó. Macías resolved to travel to the UN to bolster international awareness of the issue, and his firebrand speeches in New York contributed to Spain naming a date for both independence and general elections. In July 1968 virtually all Bubi leaders journed to the UN in New York to try and raise awareness for their cause, but the world community was uninterested in quibbling over the specifics of colonial independence. The 1960s were a time of great optimism over the future of the former African colonies, and groups that had been close to European rulers, like the Bubi, were not viewed positively.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Independence_under_Mac.C3.ADas_.281968.E2.80.931979.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_under_Macías_(1968–1979)">Independence under Macías (1968–1979)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Independence under Macías (1968–1979)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Independence from Spain was gained on 12 October 1968 and the region became the Republic of Equatorial Guinea (the date is celebrated as the country's <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_independence_days" title="List of national independence days">Independence Day</a><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup>). Macías became president in the country's <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinean_presidential_election,_1968" class="mw-redirect" title="Equatorial Guinean presidential election, 1968">only free and fair election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> The Spanish (ruled by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Franco</a>) had backed Macías in the election due to his perceived loyalty, however while on the campaign trail he had proven to be far less easy to handle than they had expected. Much of his campaigning involved visiting rural areas of Río Muni and promising young Fang that they would have the houses and wives of the Spanish if they voted for him. In the towns he had instead presented himself as the urbane leader who had bested the Spanish at the UN, and he had won in the second round of voting – greatly helped by the vote-splitting of his rivals.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The euphoria of independence became quickly overshadowed by problems emanating from the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">Nigerian Civil War</a>. Fernando Pó was inhabited by many Biafra-supporting Ibo migrant workers and many refugees from the breakaway state fled to the island, straining it to breaking point. The <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a> began running relief flights out of Equatorial Guinea, but Macías quickly became spooked and shut the flights down, refusing to allow them to fly diesel fuel for their trucks nor oxygen tanks for medical operations. Very quickly the Biafran separatists were starved into submission without international backing.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After the Public Prosecutor complained about "excesses and maltreatment" by government officials, Macías had 150 alleged coup-plotters executed in a purge on Christmas Eve 1969, all of whom happened to be political opponents.<sup id="cite_ref-tufts_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tufts-46">[44]</a></sup> Macias Nguema further consolidated his <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarian powers</a> by <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">outlawing opposition political parties</a> in July 1970 and making himself <a href="/wiki/President_for_life" title="President for life">president for life</a> in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> He broke off ties with Spain and the West. In spite of his condemnation of <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, which he deemed "<a href="/wiki/Neo-colonialism" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-colonialism">neo-colonialist</a>", Equatorial Guinea maintained very special relations with <a href="/wiki/Communist_state" title="Communist state">communist states</a>, notably <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/USSR" class="mw-redirect" title="USSR">USSR</a>. Macias Nguema signed a preferential <a href="/wiki/Trade_agreement" title="Trade agreement">trade agreement</a> and a shipping treaty with the Soviet Union. The Soviets also made loans to Equatorial Guinea.<sup id="cite_ref-aworawo103_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aworawo103-49">[47]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The shipping agreement gave the Soviets permission for a pilot <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fishery</a> development project and also a naval base at <a href="/wiki/Luba,_Equatorial_Guinea" title="Luba, Equatorial Guinea">Luba</a>. In return the USSR was to supply fish to Equatorial Guinea. China and Cuba also gave different forms of financial, military, and technical assistance to Equatorial Guinea, which got them a measure of influence there. For the USSR, there was an advantage to be gained in the <a href="/wiki/Angola_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Angola Civil War">War in Angola</a> from access to Luba base and later on to <a href="/wiki/Malabo_International_Airport" title="Malabo International Airport">Malabo International Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-aworawo103_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aworawo103-49">[47]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1974 the <a href="/wiki/World_Council_of_Churches" title="World Council of Churches">World Council of Churches</a> affirmed that large numbers of people had been murdered since 1968 in an ongoing <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_terror" class="mw-redirect" title="Reign of terror">reign of terror</a>. A quarter of the entire population had fled abroad, they said, while 'the prisons are overflowing and to all intents and purposes form one vast concentration camp'. Out of a population of 300,000, an estimated 80,000 were killed.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> Apart from allegedly committing <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a> against the ethnic minority <a href="/wiki/Bubi_people" title="Bubi people">Bubi people</a>, Macias Nguema ordered the deaths of thousands of suspected opponents, closed down churches and presided over the economy's collapse as skilled citizens and foreigners fled the country.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Obiang_.281979.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Obiang_(1979–present)">Obiang (1979–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Obiang (1979–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The nephew of Macías Nguema, <a href="/wiki/Teodoro_Obiang_Nguema_Mbasogo" title="Teodoro Obiang Nguema Mbasogo">Teodoro Obiang</a> deposed his uncle on 3 August 1979, in a bloody <i><a href="/wiki/1979_Equatorial_Guinea_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1979 Equatorial Guinea coup d'état">coup d'état</a></i>; over two weeks of civil war ensued until Nguema was captured. He was tried and executed soon afterward, with Obiang succeeding him as a less bloody, but still authoritarian president.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1995 <a href="/wiki/Mobil" title="Mobil">Mobil</a>, an American oil company, discovered oil in Equatorial Guinea. The country subsequently experienced rapid economic development, but earnings from the country's oil wealth have not reached the population and the country ranks low on the UN human development index. Some 20% of children die before age 5 and more than 50% of the population lacks access to clean <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Teodoro_Obiang_Nguema_Mbasogo" title="Teodoro Obiang Nguema Mbasogo">President Teodoro Obiang</a> is widely suspected of using the country's oil wealth to enrich himself<sup id="cite_ref-global_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-global-54">[52]</a></sup> and his associates. In 2006, Forbes estimated his personal wealth at $600 million.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2011, the government announced it was planning a new capital for the country, named <a href="/wiki/Oyala" class="mw-redirect" title="Oyala">Oyala</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> The city was renamed <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_de_la_Paz" title="Ciudad de la Paz">Ciudad de la Paz</a> (<i>"City of Peace"</i>) in 2017. </p>
      <p>As of February&nbsp;2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Obiang is Africa's second-longest serving dictator after <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Paul_Biya" title="Paul Biya">Paul Biya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equatorial_Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Equatorial_Guinea" title="Politics of Equatorial Guinea">Politics of Equatorial Guinea</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;">

      </div>
